          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789774378317" href="/book/show/7882218">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7154074-mai-ahmd?shelf=%D9%81%D9%84%D8%B3%D9%81%D8%A9">فلسفة</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer280739050" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13729468188798996541"><b>...بداية يجب أن أذكر أني تعرفت على بيسوا مصادفة لم أكن أعرف هذا الشاعر ولم أسمع عنه قبل أن أمد يدي في معرض الكتاب على ديوانه رباعيات والذي كتب فيه أرق وأعذب قصائد الحب وهي القصائد الوحيدة التي كتبها في الحب لقد شدتني عذوبة هذا الشاعر وروحه الشفيفة وتصويراته الرقيقة كأن يقول <br/>المريول الذي أخذته من الدرج <br/>أليس له جيب <br/>لأضع نفسي فيه <br/>لأكون قربك دائما ! <br/><br/><br/>ويقول <br/><br/><br/>أدرتِ وجهك ِ حين <br/>هممت أن أقول لكِ في النهاية <br/>بأنك ِ لو أدرت ِ وجهك <br/>لن يضايقني ذلك ! <br/><br/><br/>أيضا <br/>أجيء وحدي إلى الشاطىء <br/>أجي إلى الشاطىء , أفكر <br/>بالحركة التي</b></span>
  <span id="freeText13729468188798996541" style="display:none"><b>...بداية يجب أن أذكر أني تعرفت على بيسوا مصادفة لم أكن أعرف هذا الشاعر ولم أسمع عنه قبل أن أمد يدي في معرض الكتاب على ديوانه رباعيات والذي كتب فيه أرق وأعذب قصائد الحب وهي القصائد الوحيدة التي كتبها في الحب لقد شدتني عذوبة هذا الشاعر وروحه الشفيفة وتصويراته الرقيقة كأن يقول <br />المريول الذي أخذته من الدرج <br />أليس له جيب <br />لأضع نفسي فيه <br />لأكون قربك دائما ! <br /><br /><br />ويقول <br /><br /><br />أدرتِ وجهك ِ حين <br />هممت أن أقول لكِ في النهاية <br />بأنك ِ لو أدرت ِ وجهك <br />لن يضايقني ذلك ! <br /><br /><br />أيضا <br />أجيء وحدي إلى الشاطىء <br />أجي إلى الشاطىء , أفكر <br />بالحركة التي تثيرها تنورتك <br />عندما تجيئين أنتِ إلى الشاطىء ! <br /><br /><br />وهكذا نشأت بيني وبين بيسوا علاقة حب فطاردت حرفه حتى وجدتُ له ديوانه حارس القطيع وبحثت أيضا فوجدت له أيضا ديوان آخر بترجمة المهدي أخريف نسخة إلكترونية وأخذت أعبّ من نهل هذا الشاعر الممتلىء بالإحساس حتى قرأت مؤخرأ عن كتاب اللاطمأنينة وهو عبارة عن شذرات نصوص وتأملات , والحمد لله تيسر لي الحصول على الكتاب من خلال إحدى الصديقات التي جلبته لي من مصر ..<br /><br /><br />كتاب اللاطمأنينة عبارة عن مقاطع أطلق عليها بيسوا الوضع الراهن للاكينونة , وهي ليست يوميات كما يعتقد البعض إنما كما يقول المترجم هي حفريات في الذات , كتاب من الإحساس والتأمل الذي يمضي بالإفكار إلى أبعد حافاتها القصوى مطلا بقهقة واهنة على هاويات لم يختبر قرارها سوى بيسوا , هو كتاب نثر ولكنه كتب بلغة شاعرية وقد استغرق ظهوره للنور أكثر من عشر سنوات بسبب العراقيل التي وجدها المترجم , ويذكر أن الكتاب لم ينشر في حياة بيسوا بل هو مادة خام لم يتمكن بيسوا من التعديل أو الإضافة أو تحقيق الكتاب , إن الجهد الذي بذله المترجم فاق كل حدود التصور , كما يجب الإشارة إلى إن هناك الكثير من المقاطع الغير مكتملة والتي إضطر المترجم إلى ترك فراغ يشبه هذا ( --- ) إشارة إلى أن جزء من النص مفقود أو () إشارة إلى أن هناك إضافة من الناشر ..<br /><br /><br />يقول بيسوا في تقديمه لكتابه: واحدة من مآسي الروح الكبرى أن تنفذ عملاً ثم تدرك، فور انتهائك منه، أنه ليس من الجودة في شيء، تكبر المأساة خصوصاً عندما يدرك المرء أن هذا العمل هو قصارى ما يستطيع بذله، ولكن، أن تكتب عملاً، وأنت تعرف مسبقاً أنه مختل وناقص، وأنت تكتبه، مختلاً وناقصاً فهذه ذروة العذاب والذل الروحيين، لست راضياً عن القصائد التي أكتبها الآن فحسب؛ بل أعرف إني لن أرضى أيضاً عن القصائد التي سأكتبها في المستقبل، أعرف هذا فلسفياً وبلحم جسدي، من استشراف ضبابي لا أدرى من أين استقيته، فإذن، لماذا أستمر بالكتابة؟ لأني لم أتعلم بعد المزاولة التامة للتخلي الذي أعظ به، لم أتمكن بعد من التخلي عن ميلي إلى الشعر والنثر، عليّ بالكتابة، وكأنني أنفذ عقوبة ما، والعقوبة القصوى هي أن أعرف أن كل ما أكتبه عديم الجدوى، ناقص ويفتقد إلى اليقين<br /><br /><br />بيسوا اختلق شخصية برنارد سوارش الذي تسبب له وللقارىء الكثير من الإرباك فهو كثيرا ما يتساءل هل هو أنا آخر له، أم مجرد شخصية أدبية، والملاحظ إن هناك الكثير من الحوارات التي دارت بينه وبين هذه الشخصية المختلقة حتى إنها أحيانا تطغى على وجوده حتى يختفي هو ويبرز الآخر<br />و يوضح الناقد أنخيل كريسبو أن شخصية سوارش اخترعها بيسوا في أيامه الأخيرة،وهي تبدو مكتوبة بالأسلوب الأنضج والأكثر تطوراً، مما يستدعي التفكير بأنها كُتبت خلال أيامه الأخيرة ..<br /><br /><br />إن المتأمل في حياة بيسوا يرى أن أغلب كتاباته تميل للعزلة وتكشف عن معاناة حقيقية عاشها الشاعر وهو يتسكع في شوارع لشبونه أو وهو يطل على العالم من خلال نافذته في الطابق الرابع <br />( ثمة شيء يغمني , قلق مجهول رغبة غير محددة في شيء غير محدد , إحساسي بأنني حي ربما جاءني متأخرا , وعندما أطللت من النافذة العالية جدا على الشارع الذي رأيته بدون أن اراه , أحسستني فجأة واحدا من الخرق الرطبة المخصصة لتنظيف أشياء متسخة توضع على النافذة لتجف , لكنها تُنسى ملفوفة على الجدار الذي تمضي ملطخة إياه ببطء ! )<br /><br /><br /><br />إن هذا الكتاب لا يمكن أن تتماشى معه أي تسمية أخرى هو كتاب خالي من الطمأنينة التي يبحث عن أي إنسان على وجه الأرض ستصلك أحاسيس الشاعر من خلال حرفه السوداوي وربما ينقلك لحالة من الكآبة والحزن والشعور باللامعنى !<br /><br /></b><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13729468188798996541" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_236277231" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/236277231" />
    <a title="Aubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472535045p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/236277231?book_show_action=true">Nov 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781878972279" href="/book/show/759914.The_Book_of_Disquiet">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=5-star">5-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=books-are-the-best-invention">books-are-the-best-invention</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=portuguese">portuguese</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1-read-on-hand">1-read-on-hand</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2012">r-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer236277231" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8415167331314427039">A trifecta of absolute favorites? Well, not favorites. Existence definers, then. I'll have to say though, this self-discovery wasn't nearly as enjoyable as it was with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31548.Of_Human_Bondage' title='Of Human Bondage by W. Somerset Maugham' rel='nofollow noopener'>Of Human Bondage</a> or <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/88077.The_Magic_Mountain' title='The Magic Mountain by Thomas Mann' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Magic Mountain</a>. I'd turn a page, and there was one of my innermost thoughts, laid out on the page in all its proud solitude.<br/><br/>Solitude. It takes one intimate with this word and all its facets of life to appreciate this book. The author created an entire world of characters in himself, seeing no journey more i</span>
  <span id="freeText8415167331314427039" style="display:none">A trifecta of absolute favorites? Well, not favorites. Existence definers, then. I'll have to say though, this self-discovery wasn't nearly as enjoyable as it was with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31548.Of_Human_Bondage" title="Of Human Bondage by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">Of Human Bondage</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/88077.The_Magic_Mountain" title="The Magic Mountain by Thomas Mann" rel="nofollow noopener">The Magic Mountain</a>. I'd turn a page, and there was one of my innermost thoughts, laid out on the page in all its proud solitude.<br /><br />Solitude. It takes one intimate with this word and all its facets of life to appreciate this book. The author created an entire world of characters in himself, seeing no journey more important than that of the one into oneself. I have not created my own host of fellow souls, but I am intimately familiar with the ever present malaise, the hesitance toward human interaction, the constant worry over ones reputation with others (strangers on the streets to valued friends to all levels of knowing). Ever present dreaming, ever present distraction, ever present evaluation alongside analysis of the self. Proclaiming the uselessness of everything, yet never making the final step. Dreaming of the novel yet knowing that the novel will never happen so long as the familiar remains itself. Playing mental games to deal with the thinking, the feeling, the hopes and desires suffocated in a soul with myriad reasons for not chasing them.What is the cause of this? What chemical pattern of brain influenced by the combination of genes sinks the self down into introversion, into deep safe waters, always craving yet disdaining yet loving yet loathing the concept and existence of the sun. Who knows.<br /><br />I have not gone as deep as this one here though, and I would have to say that this is better. I don't envy his existence. I see what he has written and can claim multitudes of passages as original thoughts, made by myself upon analysis of our similar existences. There is a quote that says loneliness conveys the sorrow of being alone, while solitude expresses the joy. I look at this book, twenty years of solitude, and I see no solution beyond that of a mindset that I am unwilling to embrace. Falling back on religion is not something I plan on doing anytime soon. Nor will I turn the pain of loneliness into pleasure. I am not so vindictive against humanity as of yet. <br /><br />This book defines a patch of my soul, but I will not let that patch define me; reading this is just another milestone in my path of figuring out my self, and how to allow myself to live as I desire. A wake up call, of sorts. It will be worth rereading if I ever start sinking into this train of thought; it'll definitely be a sign that I need a change, a vacation of sorts. I haven't yet lost the appreciation of the novel, and I'll be using this book as a reminder of what can happen if I ever do so. A resource against calamity indeed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8415167331314427039" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3646909330037032277"><br/><br/>"The magical power of words, whether isolated or brought together to form a musical chord, full of intimate resonances and meanings that diverge even as they converge, the pomp of sentences placed in between the meanings of other sentences, malicious vestiges, hopeful woods, and nothing but the peaceful pools in the childhood gardens of my subterfuges… Thus, between the high walls of absurd audacity, among the lines of trees and the startled shivers of things withering, someone other than me wou</span>
  <span id="freeText3646909330037032277" style="display:none"><br /><br />"The magical power of words, whether isolated or brought together to form a musical chord, full of intimate resonances and meanings that diverge even as they converge, the pomp of sentences placed in between the meanings of other sentences, malicious vestiges, hopeful woods, and nothing but the peaceful pools in the childhood gardens of my subterfuges… Thus, between the high walls of absurd audacity, among the lines of trees and the startled shivers of things withering, someone other than me would hear from sad lips the confession denied to the more insistent. Not even if the knights were to ride back down the road visible from atop the castle wall would there be more peace in the Castle of the Last Lost Men, where once lances clashed and clanged in the courtyard, nor would anyone recall another name on this side of the road, apart from the one that used to enchant us nightly, like the tale about the Moorish ladies, and the child who died afterwards from life and wonder."</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_462244891" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/462244891" />
    <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye"><img alt="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594424898p2/5022264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/462244891?book_show_action=true">Nov 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye">Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781846687358" href="/book/show/6897011-the-book-of-disquiet">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=pessoa">pessoa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=read-2015">read-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews-4-stars">reviews-4-stars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer462244891" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9184115987599783115"><b>Like a Version (Touched for the Very First Time)</b><br/><br/>This is an exceptional book or work or whatever you want to call it.<br/><br/>However, ultimately, I found it both fascinating and (just a little bit) frustrating.<br/><br/>One source of frustration is that, upon completing it, I discovered that the version I had read (translated by Margaret Jull Costa) was 262 pages, whereas the Penguin Classics version (translated by Richard Zenith) is 544 pages. I hate it when this happens. I feel duped. Nothing had forewarned me o</span>
  <span id="freeText9184115987599783115" style="display:none"><b>Like a Version (Touched for the Very First Time)</b><br /><br />This is an exceptional book or work or whatever you want to call it.<br /><br />However, ultimately, I found it both fascinating and (just a little bit) frustrating.<br /><br />One source of frustration is that, upon completing it, I discovered that the version I had read (translated by Margaret Jull Costa) was 262 pages, whereas the Penguin Classics version (translated by Richard Zenith) is 544 pages. I hate it when this happens. I feel duped. Nothing had forewarned me of this possibility.<br /><br />Readers have different views on the merits of the translations. I was perfectly happy with the quality of the text in the version I read (plus I love the cover!). However, the sheer difference in length has made me question whether and, if so, how much, text was omitted from the earlier version. <br /><br />This might not be such a big deal. If indeed there is a difference in the amount of text, I imagine that much of it might have replicated what was included in the original version. There is already considerable duplication in the work. Alternatively, it might have consisted of complementary material, the absence of which did not detract from the content of the original version.<br /><br />Regardless, the fact that this issue occurred at all points to another cause of my frustration.<br /><br /><b>Fragments from under the Floorboards</b><br /><br />Both versions of the work have been presented to the reader as if it was a novel. It's even suggested that it's one of the great Modernist novels of the 20th Century.<br /><br />I don't want to be precious about the definition of the word "novel". As far as I'm concerned, if the author thinks their work is a novel, that's good enough for me.<br /><br />However, here, the work as a whole (in whatever version) has been assembled by a team of experts and editors from a trunk full of hundreds or thousands of fragments.<br /><br />It's not clear whether Pessoa regarded the project as a novel. Nor is it clear whether he regarded any version or part of the project as a finished work. Or in what order he would have presented the work or novel, had he finished it.<br /><br />The sequence in which the fragments have been ordered (presumably, from a selection) is actually a triumph of sympathetic editing.<br /><br />However, I'm not sure whether, if the author intended the work to be a novel, it would have looked anything like what I read.<br /><br />To the extent that its formal concerns might qualify it as a work of Modernist fiction, you have to ask whether they derive from the author or his editors.<br /><br /><b>Textual Personae</b><br /><br />I am nevertheless equally fascinated by the metafictional pretence behind its submission to the reader.<br /><br />The work purports to be the product of the heteronymic author, Bernardo Soares, a figment of Pessoa's imagination.<br /><br />Soares was not just a pseudonym for Pessoa writing as himself. He was a fully-fledged persona, clearly differentiated from Pessoa and many other heteronyms he used to imagine and write other discrete aspects of his work. <br /><br />Thus, the existence of the heteronym allowed Pessoa to fully explore aspects of his imagination, aesthetics and philosophy, without any limitation inferred from its ultimate source in the one person. (Mind you, Pessoa acknowledged that Soares most resembled his true self [<i>"me minus reason and affectivity"</i>], to the extent there might only have been one.)<br /><br />The result is that this work is not just fragmentary in its own right. It is the product of a fragmented author.<br /><br />Whether or not it was ever intended to be a novel (by either of its <i>"authors"</i>), the work itself (or at least the analysis of it) fits within the concerns of Modernism, if not Post-Modernism (which I maintain is a branch of Modernism, a sub-movement, not a separate movement).<br /><br /><b>Melancholy Nihilism</b><br /><br />The fragmentation also reflects the philosophical concerns of the author(s).<br /><br />Ultimately, I sense that this is a philosophical work, rather than a fictional work.<br /><br />It's a fragmented, but ultimately comprehensive and systematic, contemplation of the narrator's world and his place in it. The narrator is a thinker, not a man of action. Little happens in the work other than thinking about the self and its relationship with others and the world. It's not quite solipsistic, because the narrator acknowledges the existence of the outside world. However, for him, his own mind is of paramount concern.<br /><br />The editors have assembled the fragments in a thematic way, even though the same themes appear multiple times in the finished text. It could equally have been organised a different way. Or distilled into a short work of melancholy wisdom.<br /><br />The work is a testament to inveterate egoism, miserabilism and misanthropy. Yet, it's been fashioned into a comprehensible philosophy.<br /><br />If sub-headings were added as signposts, it would make a fantastic guide to nihilism or whatever you want to call this particular philosophy. I am reluctant to describe it as Existentialism, because of the apparent lack of Humanism.<br /><br />Whatever you call it, it purports to be a philosophy made by a melancholy person for melancholy people, to the extent that there is any concern for others at all. <br /><br />Its closest fictional parallel is Dostoyevsky's <i>"Notes from Underground".</i><br /><br /><b>Prepossessing Aphorisms</b><br /><br />It's hard to say how much readers are expected to distance themselves from the ostensible authors or their philosophy. <br /><br />Even if it's serious, it would be ironic if only sad or self-pitying readers related to or enjoyed this work.<br /><br />Its beauty resides in the quality of writing, which can be enjoyed by all readers with a metaphysical bent.<br /><br />Indeed, if all philosophy were conceived and written this lyrically, it wouldn't be the preserve of desk-bound, incomprehensible polysyllabists and LL.B.'s that it seems to have become.<br /><br />This work is as literary and aphoristic as Friedrich Nietzsche, Oscar Wilde and George Bernard Shaw.<br /><br />You can understand and enjoy it, even if you don't agree or sympathise with its underlying philosophy.<br /><br />Ultimately, for this reason alone, it is a creative work, if not necessarily fiction.<br /><br />Still, there is always the possibility that the fiction lies in the creation of a non-fiction work by a fictitious author, narrator or character (as ably assisted by the experts and editors)! It's hard to tell whether the metaphysics is bona fide or purely metafictional.<br /><br />The whole text or philosophy might even be ironic. Who knows? Perhaps Johnny Marr could put it to music!<br /><br />At least this prospect makes it good for a laugh or maybe even a dance. Roger Wilco Foxtrot!<br /><br /><br /><br /><b>"A Mercator Projection of the Soul"<br />[An Assemblage of Aphorisms]</b><br /><br />Below are some aphorisms that map the metaphysical journey of the work:<br /><br /><b>Life and the Abyss:</b><br /><br /><i>Life would be unbearable if we were truly conscious of it. (212)</i><br /><br /><i>
  <b>If there is one thing life gives us, apart from life itself, and for which we must thank the gods, it is the gift of not knowing ourselves: of not knowing ourselves and of not knowing one another. The human soul is an abyss of viscous darkness...no one would love themselves if they really knew themselves...(236)</b>
</i><br /><br /><b>Nihilism and Illusion:</b><br /><br /><i>In order not to demean ourselves in our own eyes, it is enough that we should become accustomed to harbouring no ambitions, passions, desires, hopes, impulses or feelings of restlessness.(186)</i><br /><br /><i>
  <b>Once we believe this world to be merely an illusion and a phantasm, we are then free to consider everything that happens to us as a dream, something that only pretended to because we were asleep.(220)</b>
</i><br /><br /><b>Death and Inaction:</b><br /><br /><i>I've become a character in a book, a life already dead. Quite against my wishes, what I feel is felt in order for me to write it down.(139)</i><br /><br /><i>
  <b>Living seems to me a metaphysical mistake on the part of matter, an oversight on the part of inaction.(114)</b>
</i><br /><br /><b>Looking and Feeling:</b><br /><br /><i>For me, humanity is one vast decorative motif, existing through one's eyes and ears and through psychological emotion. I demand nothing more from life than to be a spectator of it. I demand nothing more from myself than to be a spectator of life.(198)</i><br /><br /><i>
  <b>I am an endlessly sensitive photographic plate. In me every tiny detail is recorded and magnified in order to form part of a whole. I concern myself only with myself. For me the external world is pure sensation. I never forget what I feel.(178)</b>
</i><br /><br /><b>Egoism and Disquiet:</b><br /><br /><i>That is my morality or my metaphysics or me myself: a passer-by in everything, even in my own soul. I belong to nothing, I desire nothing, I am nothing except an abstract centre of impersonal sensations, a sentient mirror fallen from the wall but still turned to reflect the diversity of the world. I don't care if this makes me happy or unhappy, and I don't much care.(151)</i><br /><br /><i>
  <b>The generation to which I belong was born into a world devoid of certainty for anyone possessed of both an intellect and a heart...the world into which we were born had no security to offer us as regards religion, no anchor as regards morality, no stability as regards politics. We were born into a state of anguish, both metaphysical and moral, and of political disquiet.(206)</b>
</i><br /><br /><b>Love and Onanism:</b><br /><br /><i>We never love anyone. We love only our idea of what someone is like. We love an idea of our own; in short, it is ourselves that we love...The onanist may be an abject creature but in truth he is the logical expression of the lover. He is the only one who neither disguises nor deludes himself.(218)</i><br /><br /><i>
  <b>To love is merely to grow tired of being alone: it is therefore both cowardice and a betrayal of ourselves (it is vitally important that we should not love).(240)</b>
</i><br /><br /><b>Futility and Nothingness:</b><br /><br /><i>The one reason we get on together is that we know nothing about one another.</i><br /><br /><i>
  <b>Love disturbs and wearies, action dissipates and disappoints, no one truly knows how to know, and thinking confuses everything. Better then to put a stop to all our desires and hopes, to our futile attempts to explain the world, or to any foolish ambitions to change or govern it. Everything is nothing...(242)</b>
</i><br /><br /><b>Tedium and Worthlessness:</b><br /><br /><i>Tedium is not a sickness brought on by the boredom of having nothing to do, but the worse sickness of feeling that nothing is worth doing.(91)</i><br /><br /><i>
  <b>Tedium is boredom with the world, the malaise of living, the weariness of having lived; in truth, tedium is the feeling in one's flesh of the endless emptiness of things.(122)</b>
</i><br /><br /><b>Silence and Emptiness:</b><br /><br /><i>I feel this because I feel nothing. I think this because this is all nothing. Nothing, nothing, just part of the night and the silence and of whatever emptiness, negativity and inconstancy I share with them, the space that exists between me and me, a thing mislaid by some god...(262)</i><br /><br /><br /><br /><b>ADDED EXTRAS:</b><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br /><br /><i><b>["Just as Some Music Makes Me Want to Dance,<br />Some Words Make Me Want to Play"]</b></i> <br /><br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><br />Rather than doing updates, the text prompted me to write some mock "verse", inspired by either Pessoa's words (in which case I have simply versified them more or less intact) or the tone of his text.<br /><br />I also wrote a story that I placed in comment #1 in the thread.<br /><br /><br /><b>The Semi-Heteronym (Me Minus Reason and Affectivity)</b><br /><br />As luck would have it,<br />If I was there, so was this<br />Particular man.<br /><br /><br /><b>Transmitted by Concupiscence</b><br /><br />Once I was<br />Innocent,<br />But that was<br />Only once<br />And many<br />Years ago. <br /><br /><br /><b>On Making a Mockery</b><br /><br />It doesn't matter<br />If you laugh at me,<br />For I too have scorn<br />In my armoury.<br /><br /><br /><b>Just Doodles Haiku</b><br /><br />These are the doodles<br />Of my incomprehension<br />Of my consciousness.<br /><br /><br /><b>Lost in Analysis</b><br /><br />I lost myself<br />In abstract thought,<br />But found myself<br />Once more again<br />In the pages<br />I wrested from<br />Oblivion.<br /><br /><br /><b>Heidegger's Children<br />[In the Words of Bernardo Soares]</b><br /><br />Autumn will take<br />Ev'ry single<br />Philosophy<br />That Heidegger's<br />Drowsy children<br />Of the abyss<br />Play at making.<br /><br /><br /><b>Anticipatory Retrospection<br />[In the Words of Bernardo Soares]</b><br /><br />I remember him now<br />As I will in the future<br />With the nostalgia I know<br />I will feel for him then.<br /><br /><br /><b>We Find One Another Wanting</b><br /><br />Solitude torments us,<br />Though it's habitual.<br />Company oppresses us,<br />Despite its ritual.<br /><br /><br /><b>Never Go Too Near</b><br /><br />Know the difference<br />Between voluptuousness<br />And noble pleasure.<br /><br /><br /><b>Fragments of a Rainy Season<br />[In the Words of Bernardo Soares]</b><br /><br />These words are guesses<br />Made in the void,<br />Trembling on the brink<br />Of the deepest abyss.<br />Through them trickles<br />The plangent sound<br />Of the constant rain<br />Outside the window.<br /><br /><br /><b>What She Offers to My Eyes<br />[In the Words of Bernardo Soares]</b><br /><br />This is how I love:<br />I love with my eyes,<br />Not my fantasy.<br />I don't fantasise;<br />I don't imagine.<br />I keep whole a heart,<br />Given over to<br />Unreal destinies.<br /><br /><br /><b>A Shot in the Arm<br />[In the Words of Roger Wilco]</b><br /><br />What I once <br />Thought isn't <br />What I want <br />To believe<br />Any more.<br /><br /><br /><b>No Self-Pity Them<br />[Assembled from the Words and Thoughts of Bernardo Soares]</b><br /><br />I<br /><br />Wise men achieve<br />Their happiness<br />By making life<br />Monotonous.<br />For then, for them,<br />Tiny incidents<br />Are imbued with<br />Great significance.<br /><br />II<br /><br />Wise men protect their souls<br />With just their human senses.<br />At the onset of any sadness,<br />They assert their innocence.<br />The wise shirk the disquiet<br />Of other men's existence,<br />And defy successive tragedies<br />With consummate indifference.<br /><br /><br /><br /><b>SOUNDTRACK:</b><br /><br />See comment #2 in the thread.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9184115987599783115" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fuchsia  Groan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fuchsia  Groan" href="/user/show/14848732-fuchsia-groan">Fuchsia  Groan</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788415689485" href="/book/show/3212085-libro-del-desasosiego">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14848732-fuchsia-groan?shelf=cl%C3%A1sicos">clásicos</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14848732-fuchsia-groan?shelf=favoritos">favoritos</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2743178081" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer2506346777459916393">Tras un mes leyendo y otro dándole vueltas a lo leído, al fin me he decidido a dar por terminada esta obra inmensa, sintiendo que apenas la he empezado.<br/><br/>No sabría explicar qué es lo que me ha obsesionado de lo leído, ¿lo narrado?, ¿el autor?, ¿el personaje creado, si es que son distintos?, ¿el personaje que quizás yo misma he inventado?, ¿con lo que me he identificado?, ¿con las reflexiones que no he llegado a entender e incluso he despreciado?<br/><br/>He disfrutado muchísimo leyendo los desasosiegos de P</span>
  <span id="freeText2506346777459916393" style="display:none">Tras un mes leyendo y otro dándole vueltas a lo leído, al fin me he decidido a dar por terminada esta obra inmensa, sintiendo que apenas la he empezado.<br /><br />No sabría explicar qué es lo que me ha obsesionado de lo leído, ¿lo narrado?, ¿el autor?, ¿el personaje creado, si es que son distintos?, ¿el personaje que quizás yo misma he inventado?, ¿con lo que me he identificado?, ¿con las reflexiones que no he llegado a entender e incluso he despreciado?<br /><br />He disfrutado muchísimo leyendo los desasosiegos de Pessoa, o Soares, esta <i>historia sin vida</i>, sus pequeñas alegrías <i>(no conozco placer como el de los libros)</i>, que en realidad nunca son tales <i>(detesto la lectura)</i>, su tristeza por su “novida”, su valentía al confesarse: <i>Me sobreviene entonces un terror sarcástico de la vida, un desaliento que traspasa los límites de mi individualidad consciente. Sé que fui error y descamino, que nunca viví, que existí sólo porque llené tiempo con conciencia y pensamiento.</i><br /><br />La visión del narrador es desoladora, el mundo exterior, los demás, son algo ajeno, no merece la pena, <i>Cultivo el odio a la acción como una flor de invernadero. Presumo ante mí mismo de mi disidencia de la vida.</i> La vida se concibe como algo interno, <i>A quien, como yo, así, viviendo, no sabe tener vida, ¿qué le queda sino, como a mis escasos pares, la renuncia por modo y la contemplación por destino?</i><br /><br />Y así, solo queda la soledad, y ahí Soares cae en una contradicción de imposible solución: <i>La soledad me desola, la compañía me oprime.</i><br /><br /><i>Pedí tan poco a la vida y ese mismo poco la vida me lo negó. Un haz de parte del sol, un campo próximo, un poco de sosiego con un poco de pan, no pesarme mucho el saber que existo, y no exigir nada de los otros ni ellos nada de mí. Esto mismo me fue negado, como quien niega la limosna no por falta de buena alma, sino por tener que desabrocharse la chaqueta.</i><br /><br />Al final, no hay salida: <i>¿Hacia dónde imaginar la huida, si la celda lo es todo?</i> </span>
  <a data-text-id="2506346777459916393" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1578759380" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1578759380" />
    <a title="Julie Christine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1213607-julie-christine"><img alt="Julie Christine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445900882p2/1213607.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1578759380?book_show_action=true">Mar 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie Christine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie Christine" href="/user/show/1213607-julie-christine">Julie Christine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141183046" href="/book/show/45974.The_Book_of_Disquiet">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=bio-autobio-memoir">bio-autobio-memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=shorts">shorts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=read-2016">read-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=social-political-commentary">social-political-commentary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=writing-companions">writing-companions</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=best-of-2016">best-of-2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1578759380" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7455883766891804931">The four months it took me to read Fernando Pessoa's posthumously-published collection of thought fragments have been some of the most fraught and chrysalis-splitting days of my adult life. This book will forever be synonymous with transition and grief, exploration and longing. I could read only bits at a time, for Pessoa's struggle to understand the world and his place in it mirrored my own and my many gasps of recognition left me breathless. <br/><br/>Of course, this is not a book to be read in an order</span>
  <span id="freeText7455883766891804931" style="display:none">The four months it took me to read Fernando Pessoa's posthumously-published collection of thought fragments have been some of the most fraught and chrysalis-splitting days of my adult life. This book will forever be synonymous with transition and grief, exploration and longing. I could read only bits at a time, for Pessoa's struggle to understand the world and his place in it mirrored my own and my many gasps of recognition left me breathless. <br /><br />Of course, this is not a book to be read in an orderly fashion, within a time frame, for a singular purpose. It is meant to be read as it was written, in fragments, unending, one you can pick up at any time, turn to any page, read forwards and back and inside-out. It is a kaleidoscope of dreams and reflections, a rumination on what it means to be a writer, the terrible weight of being human. <br /><br />Of <i>The Book of Disquiet</i> writer Rabih Alameddine says, <i>"A book tells you quite a bit about its author; a great book tells you quite a bit about you. When I first encountered Disquiet, I felt like laundry — the book dunked me in pristine water, then battered and wrung me and hung me out to dry in sunshine, rejuvenated. I was forced to examine the choices I'd made, the beliefs I'd held, the loves I'd forsaken and the gods I'd worshipped.</i> Yes. This. <br /><br />Pessoa penned his musings from Lisbon over the course of many years, from WWI to his death in 1935. We are seeing a dark time in the life of a nation, in the life of a solitary man with few friends and family, working at a clerical job which offered little but time to ponder. The melancholic tone woven throughout reflects not only Europe between the wars but a particular 21st century angst, as well. How does one live in the world without being swallowed by it? I found myself longing for the type of solitude Pessoa experienced, those many decades before our lives were invaded by television and the world was cinched tight by the Internet and social media. Detaching from the world is now so very difficult. I'm romanticizing Pessoa's loneliness, to be sure, but what bliss, this silence. <br /><br />I kept a piece of paper tucked inside the book, marking the numbers and opening lines of Fragments that resonated with particular force. Thinking perhaps I'd list them here, in my review. By the end, however, the list had become unwieldy, the fragments too many. But here are a choice few . . . <br /><br /><i>* Fragment 28: A breath of music or of a dream, of something that would make me almost feel, something that would make me not think.<br /><br />* Fragment 48: Solitude devastates me. Company oppresses me.<br /><br />* Fragment 63: I feel like I'm always on the verge of waking up.<br /><br />* Fragment 93: By thinking so much, I become echo and abyss.<br /><br />* Fragment 112: We never love anyone. What we love is the idea we have of someone. It's our own concept—our own selves—that we love.<br /><br />* Fragment 152: To write is to lose myself, yes, but everyone loses himself, because everything gets lost. I, however, lose myself without any joy—not like the river flowing into the sea for which it was secretly born, but like the puddle left on the beach by the high tide, its stranded water never returning to the ocean but merely sinking into the sand. <br /><br />* Fragment 193: I am, in large measure, the selfsame prose I write. I unroll myself in sentences and paragraphs, I punctuate myself. In my arranging and rearranging of images I'm like a child using newspaper to dress up as a king, and in the way I create rhythm with a series of word I'm like a lunatic adorning my hair with dried flowers that are still alive in my dreams. <br /><br />* Fragment 194: A terrible weariness fills the soul of my heart. I feel sad because of whom I never was, and I don't know with what kind of nostalgia I miss him. I fell, with every sunset, against my hopes and certainties.<br /><br />* Fragment 231: One of the soul's great tragedies is to execute a work and then realize, once it's finished, that it's not any good.<br /><br />* Fragment 258: To have touched the feet of Christ is no excuse for mistakes in punctuation. If a man writes well only when he's drunk, then I'll tell him: Get drunk. And if he says that it's bad for his liver, I'll answer: What's your liver? A dead thing that lives while you live, whereas the poems you write live without while.</i><br /><br /><i>The Book of Disquiet</i> is a particular meditation that were it published today, would come accompanied by trigger warnings for the depressed and anxious. But even in its elegiac angst, there is beauty and comfort to be found. A treasured read I will return to again and again as I struggle to make sense of my life and my place in the world, as Pessoa did nearly a century ago. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7455883766891804931" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1296410248" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1296410248" />
    <a title="Junta" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38230974-junta"><img alt="Junta" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1447099175p2/38230974.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1296410248?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Junta" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Junta" href="/user/show/38230974-junta">Junta</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141183046" href="/book/show/45974.The_Book_of_Disquiet">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Those who prefer thought over action, solitude to company, meaning over happiness</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Junta by: </span>
      Aubrey, Dahlia, Florencia and Stian's 5-star reviews
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1296410248" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13585817850559908714"><b>
  <u>The Review of Disquiet</u>
</b><br/>Ken. O. O. Bach<br/>Edited and Translated by Junta<br/><br/><b>1</b><br/>I was born in a time when possibilities were expanding by the day. However, so did the proportion of young people who lost touch with their dignity. It seems the trend will only continue. Visible and invisible disparities.<br/><br/><b>2</b><br/>I love the idea of myself. I am proud, but not vain. I know my defects are too strong for me to love myself as much as the idea of myself.<br/><br/><b>3</b><br/>The idea of living is tedious. One is much more comfortable living</span>
  <span id="freeText13585817850559908714" style="display:none"><b>
  <u>The Review of Disquiet</u>
</b><br />Ken. O. O. Bach<br />Edited and Translated by Junta<br /><br /><b>1</b><br />I was born in a time when possibilities were expanding by the day. However, so did the proportion of young people who lost touch with their dignity. It seems the trend will only continue. Visible and invisible disparities.<br /><br /><b>2</b><br />I love the idea of myself. I am proud, but not vain. I know my defects are too strong for me to love myself as much as the idea of myself.<br /><br /><b>3</b><br />The idea of living is tedious. One is much more comfortable living inside the mind.<br /><br /><b>4</b><br />Knowing time only goes forward, towards inevitable death, yet feeling nothing.<br /><br /><b>5</b><br />As I lie in darkness at night, it seems I am capable of anything. My weaknesses can all be mended tomorrow. All I need to do is open my heart. However, the heart can only be opened in darkness.<br />The exception to this is when there is a ray of light so beautiful and innocent that white meets blue, blue meets pink, pink meets white and the whole world is bathed in every imaginable colour. <br /><br /><b>6</b><br />The happiness of a dog, the intelligence of a cat, the curiosity of a mouse. The tremor of a cherry blossom branch, the sophistication of a hydrangea, the humility of a eucalyptus tree. The winds carry our thoughts, the rain washes away our fears, and the sun warms us up from the inside.<br /><br /><b>7</b><br />Nature is not enough for the modern man. The convict with the death sentence who is as tranquil as if they are already in heaven.<br /><br /><b>8</b><br />I am still often surprised by my naiveté. It seems that an extended period of inaction cancels out the fruits of past action.<br /><br /><b>9</b><br />I have no opinions, and hence exist. It has always puzzled me how people can have so many opinions. Indifference is the first step in living inwardly.<br /><br /><b>10</b><br />If there was a spider inside of me, its web would be covering my heart. Prey is taken in, but the predator never comes out. It would become prey out of its web. <br /><br /><b>11</b><br />I don't like many things, but the things I do I will stick with, like a faithful lady beetle.<br /><br /><b>12</b><br />Just how alone are we? Just how alone do we want to be?<br /><br /><b>13</b><br />How rare it is to be attracted to the mind of another. Rarer still for the attraction to be mutual.<br /><br /><b>14</b><br />I have been awake for a handful of days this year. If my emotions are asleep, I am asleep. <br /><br /><b>15</b><br />It is so rare for me to feel profound emotion that I am worried even the most unfortunate of events will fail to move me.<br /><br /><b>16</b><br />The reliance on thought is, in its shadow, concealing a hypersensitive heart. No, it is not sensitive to many things, but to those that actually deserve our respect and love.<br /><br /><b>17</b><br />Dreaming is more interesting than anything that can happen in life, or books. The lofty possibilities of each one are combined into rich sensations.<br /><br /><b>18</b><br />I have never seen a film more exciting than a dream I might have on a lazy afternoon. Unlike the relationship between life and fiction, in which the latter may be more enticing, living inside a dream is much more real than life itself, and much more beautiful than any fiction that, at the end of the day, is not concerned with us. Reading is life's second biggest luxury. It is a shame dreaming is considered a part of life, and not the other way around.<br /><br /><b>19</b><br />Books. Paper. Trees. Air. Breath. Oxygen. Brain. Mystery. Maze. Serpent. Sword. Hilt. Power. Domination. Prejudice. Colour. Grey. Dullness. Repetition. Habit. Personality. Uniqueness. The meaning of life. ...<br /><br /><b>20</b><br />Do my chair and desk respect my writing? They have spent too much time with me that they must be apathetic.<br /><br /><b>21</b><br />Words betray our thoughts. Words are the physical value of gold. Thoughts are its intrinsic beauty. <br /><br /><b>22</b><br />Another year of no change. The things you can change are not essential to your own being.<br /><br /><b>23</b><br />The social meaningfulness of sleeping and dreaming are disproportionately low. <br /><br /><b>24</b><br />"I'm different from everyone."<br />"Oh, me too actually."<br />"That's a coincidence, so am I."<br /><br /><b>25</b><br />Turn this way<br />There are so many things I want to talk to you about<br />Though I can only meet you in my imagination any more.<br /><br /><b>26</b><br />One of my fascinations is linking the unlinkable - an acquaintance from ten years ago and a friend I made this year, a kangaroo hopping around Kyoto, philosophising in English with a close friend whom is Japanese. Dreams are where the impossible become possible.<br /><br /><b>27</b><br />Neither belonging to the mountain, nor the sea.<br /><br /><b>28</b><br />The frustration at the incongruence between my two tongues. My person lies in the boundary between the two, a boundary that will never be broken down. Growing in one language means leaving the other behind. Since time is not given to catch up, the two are journeying along two paths becoming more divergent by the day.<br /><br /><b>29</b><br />Dreams are home, life is work, reading is play.<br /><br /><b>30</b><br />There have been a handful of individuals who I have despised from the first few seconds of meeting. These persons all have one thing in common, and it is ironic that this attribute they share is what I often long for. In reality, it is unattainable.<br /><br /><b>31</b><br />Were I ever granted a flash of expressive power so great that it concentrated all art in me, I would write a eulogy to chess. A world where thought is action, one's purpose is clear, and art, science and sport all co-exist. One can comprehend the causal chain, and be transported onto a plane where they hold the power to infinite and truth. One only loses because they deserve to lose.<br /><br /><b>32</b><br />The inability to put effort into anything.<br /><br /><b>33</b><br />I feel I lost the curiosity for life quite early. My essence lies in a deep sleep in a plain of never-melting snow under the sun from a decade ago, when I realised the incompatibility between my self and the environment. New lands bring new sensations, but the decision to venture forth took too long. I'm not sure how much I lost in those plains. I believe they exist inside myself too, though it will be a long time before the snow there melts.<br /><br /><b>34</b><br />"Why did you pull the covers over yourself?"<br />"I think it's because I feel cold."<br /><br /><b>35</b><br />Like the attic above my bedroom which I have never ventured to explore (the square opening is as good as forgotten), I am still optimistic about change. It is just that I am pessimistic about how receptive I am to change.<br /><br /><b>36</b><br />I have always been a great fan of myself. Thoughts are not published or broadcast, but I am always writing the draft.<blockquote>...Today I'm an ascetic in my religion of myself. A cup of coffee, a cigarette and my dreams can substitute quite well for the universe and its stars, for work, love, and even beauty and glory. I need virtually no stimulants. I have opium enough in my soul.</blockquote><b>37</b><br />Reading is an instance I am open to the world, since I am feeling more than thinking. I do not bother with interpretations, only impressions.<br /><br /><b>38</b><br />My apathy has also infected my memory, so I am losing my past at an impressive rate. I am used to it, and it does not bother me so much. I am the same old person from year to year, but every moment I am also becoming someone new.<br /><br /><b>39</b><br />Perhaps in the not-too-distant future, we will be living in our minds, thanks to technology. As much as I live in my mind already, I don't entirely embrace this future. Living in the mind is a voluntary privilege, luxury and virtue that should not be accessible to everyone.<br /><br /><b>40</b><br />"What do you want for Christmas?"<br />"My fermented thoughts from ten years ago."<br /><br /><b>41</b><br />I have always been fascinated by people who can show their feelings and opinions so openly. The concept of acting, becoming another person is wonderful, but I could not bear other people watching this psychological rendezvous.<br /><br /><b>42</b><br />An excess of confidence brings more results than a lack of confidence. However, the latter is tragic and beautiful.<br /><br /><b>43</b><br />Worldly rations of wisdom cannot be consciously acquired after a certain age in youth.<br /><br /><b>44</b><br />I want to combine the notes of a piano with the lake I often drive past. The smell of the lawn with a passionate discussion over whisky. The translucence of a window with a mental exercise. The taste of sake with the crescent moon. The beauty of prose with the mediocrity of people around me. Whole new worlds will open up when distant planes merge onto one. <br /><br /><b>45</b><br />Only relying on intuition on Monday. Principles on Tuesday. Taste on Wednesday. Kindness on Thursday. Indifference on Friday. Perverseness on Saturday. Optimism on Sunday.<br /><br /><b>46</b><br />I often refrain from speaking something out aloud if most people around could have said the same thing. <br /><br /><b>47</b><br />Flawed perfectionism, realistic idealism and melancholy optimism. <br /><br /><b>48</b><br />Humanity still seems to be the norm, or people would have killed each other off long ago. It is a blessing to find like-minded people because most of humanity is disagreeable or distasteful.<br /><br /><b>49</b><br />I have internalised and rationalised everything to such an extent that it's a wonder how most people give me respect for my exterior.<br /><br /><b>50</b><br />"Can I be your friend?"<br />"Only if you can enjoy this awkward silence with me."<br /><br /><b>51</b><br />"Today is the first day of the rest of your life."<br />"It will be your last if you keep up with those statements."<br /><br /><b>52</b><br />I am content with the friends I have and the things I enjoy, that the rate of finding new ones is decreasing by the year. Quantity of quality over quality of quantity.<br /><br /><b>53</b><br />"Would you rather be remembered as missing in action, or missing inaction?"<br /><br /><b>54</b><br />I am amazed at how big religion is in this world.<br /><br /><b>55</b><br />Each letter of each letter, traversing the seas.<br /><br /><b>56</b><br />Being strong and not being weak are two entirely different things. It is a question of purpose.<br /><br /><b>57</b><br />Imagining worlds where psychological conventions, power relations and social structures are completely different from reality. Do you want to make the world a better place, or be the best you can be? Both, or neither?<br /><br /><b>58</b><br />I do not mind travelling, but they have been a means to an end. I can journey around the whole universe in my mind in the comfort of home. <br /><br /><b>59</b><br />Contradictions in personality can either be pitiful, or lovely.<br /><br /><b>60</b><br />Variegated stupidity.<br /><br /><b>61</b><br />One sign of loving someone is that with them, you'd want to do things that you would usually detest or avoid at any cost.<br /><br /><b>62</b><br />I appreciate someone doing something for me a lot more than receiving something materially. I am selfish, and am happy with the rare purchases I make myself. I know what I want and don't want. Thus, what someone may give me blindly is appreciated but not necessary.<br /><br /><b>63</b><br />The beautiful oceans of objectivity and rationality. The Tropic of Spontaneity divides them.<br /><br /><b>64</b><br />"You know, you are pretty special."<br />"I was going to tell you the same thing."<br /><br /><b>65</b><br />Never having a Plan A, only contingent paths.<br /><br /><b>66</b><br />"Hey, I never thought you would dance like this."<br />"Well, this is a place for dancing."<br /><br /><b>67</b><br />Did cavemen cringe?<br /><br /><b>68</b><br />Perhaps there needs to be some difference for balance.<blockquote>...After they go their separate ways, each marrying someone else (since they think too much alike to marry each other), if one day they happen to look at these pages, I think they recognise what they never said and will be grateful to me for so accurately interpreting not only what they really are but also what they never wished to be nor ever knew they were...</blockquote><b>69</b><br />Loneliness, Intelligence, Fecundity, Eloquence.<br /><br /><b>70</b><br />If writing is a form of masturbation, how can literary sex be achieved? Taking turns is unaesthetic, but there is only space for one pen on the line.<br /><br /><b>71</b><br />I'm aware that things have tended to go well when I have successfully ceased overthinking. A narrow comfort zone.<br /><br /><b>72</b><br />Impressions of episodes are all that remain. It's a shame there is no 'Pause' function. On the crucial level, the chances of reaching a checkpoint can be 50-50.<br /><br /><b>73</b><br />"I have never felt this way before."<br /><br /><b>74</b><br />Monotone<br />Baritone<br />Anglophone<br />Miscommunication-prone.<br /><br /><b>75</b><br />"I'm going to be lonely without you."<br /><br /><b>76</b><br />Certain characters in stories by DFW and Murakami are my favourite. They are the most similar to me.<br /><br /><b>77</b><br />The last photo together in the hotel lobby.<br /><br /><b>78</b><br />"What would you like for your side dish, chivalry or commitment?"<br /><br /><b>79</b><br />I used to dislike children for they are illogical and lack objectivity. Thankfully I do not mind them so much now.<br /><br /><b>80</b><br />"What talent do you wish you had?"<br />"The capacity for hard work."<br /><br /><b>81</b><br />I miss change.<br /><br /><b>82</b><br />A stanza blossoms in spring<br />A note resounds above in summer<br />A sketch survives through autumn<br />A melody is unearthed in winter<br /><br /><b>83</b><br />"What is your dream?"<br /><br /><b>84</b><br />I want to write my own story one day. At the moment I'm incapable of writing a few lines. For now I shall stick with reading.<br /><br /><b>85</b><br />I occasionally experience déjà vu, dreaming the scene and living it months or years later.<br /><br /><b>86</b><br />In terms of physical appearance, which is the real self? The one in solitude, the one in the mirror, the one in the photograph or the one in the mind?<br /><br /><b>87</b><br />"In the 22nd century, riddles and wordplay became weapons of mass destruction."<br /><br /><b>88</b><br />"Stop thinking. You're doing it again."<br /><br /><b>89</b><br />Teenage life is suffering for those who grow up too slow, those who can't open themselves up and those who think too much. <br /><br /><b>90</b><br />Wallowing in regret used to be my preferred method of self-flagellation. Now I read instead and wallow in indifference.<br /><br /><b>91</b><br />I'm only intelligent in certain aspects. In others I'm just good at hiding my ineptitude, or avoid them altogether. However, I do look down on others who behave stupidly by choice.<br /><br /><b>92</b><br />"What is your greatest achievement in life?"<br />"Keeping a dignified presence throughout."<br /><br /><b>93</b><br />I want to write something only I can<br />Do something for the world only I can<br />Think about the world in a way only I can<br />Live in this world sometimes alone, sometimes with others<br />I've been fortunate so far. Things could be a lot worse.<br /><br /><b>94</b><br />I've found that my personalities across life and its most cherished facets (as a chessplayer, as a reader etc.) are all absolutely identical. This is a natural occurrence, but is worth analysing.<br /><br /><b>95</b><br />Eyes are beautiful windows to the soul.<br /><br /><b>96</b><br />If a creature can live both underwater and on land, how do they decide how much time they allot to the respective habitats? <br /><br /><b>97</b><br />To think about everything, to analyse everything. To place in the realms of exact science the hum of a passerby, the glance of a waitress, the gaiety of a retiree, the hesitancy of an acquaintance, the struggles of a friend, the concerns of a lover.<br /><br /><b>98</b><br />The heart, the mind, the soul. The fountain, the cave, the sky. The waves, the sand, the tide. The piercing sunshine, the stuttering rain, the air we breathe. The future, the past, the present. The girlfriend, the fiancée, the soulmate. The chaos, the structure, the universe.<br /><br /><b>99</b><br />"I'm sorry."<br /><br /><b>100</b><br />"I look forward to the day we meet again.<br /><br />Sincerely,<br />__________"<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />December 31, 2015</span>
  <a data-text-id="13585817850559908714" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1384719?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Oriana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Oriana" href="/user/show/30800-oriana">Oriana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781878972279" href="/book/show/759914.The_Book_of_Disquiet">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30800-oriana?shelf=dabbling">dabbling</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1384719" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4918516303167947001">I can only speak of this book in hushed, reverential tones. This is one of my most, most, most favorite books, which I've been reading for years and still have not finished. It's like an endless diary of daily life, written by the strangest, most deleriously unhappy (but sometimes happy), brilliant (but sometimes simple), intensely thoughtful old man. <br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7816.Fernando_Pessoa' title='Fernando Pessoa' rel='nofollow noopener'>Pessoa</a> is best known for writing poetry using "heteronyms," meaning that essentially he had multiple personalities who all were writers. He never </span>
  <span id="freeText4918516303167947001" style="display:none">I can only speak of this book in hushed, reverential tones. This is one of my most, most, most favorite books, which I've been reading for years and still have not finished. It's like an endless diary of daily life, written by the strangest, most deleriously unhappy (but sometimes happy), brilliant (but sometimes simple), intensely thoughtful old man. <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7816.Fernando_Pessoa" title="Fernando Pessoa" rel="nofollow noopener">Pessoa</a> is best known for writing poetry using "heteronyms," meaning that essentially he had multiple personalities who all were writers. He never intended to publish <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45974.The_Book_of_Disquiet__Penguin_Classics_" title="The Book of Disquiet (Penguin Classics) by Fernando Pessoa" rel="nofollow noopener">The Book of Disquiet</a>
</i> (or so I've heard); when he died, he left a trunk in his apartment filled with a billion little scraps of paper detailing his observations on everything. Then someone put them in some kind of order and made a stunning book. <br /><br />I think each edition of the book has them in a different order, but get the Exact Change edition, if you're gonna get it. Their books are so gorgeous (I have a whole shelf of only Exact Change books, because any other books put next to them look shabby by comparison), and I'm pretty sure they need your money. Plus that's the edition I have, so it must be the best.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4918516303167947001" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/878211" />
    <a title="Heather" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/69390-heather"><img alt="Heather" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/878211?book_show_action=true">Apr 25, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Heather" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Heather" href="/user/show/69390-heather">Heather</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781852427580" href="/book/show/45973.The_Book_of_Disquiet">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">those who </span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer878211" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15668854765939446038">There are many things i enjoy about this book.<br/>For starters, you can easily put this book down, forget<br/>about it, concentrate your efforts on something else and come back to it and can start off right where you left it.<br/>There isn't exactly a "plot" that needs to be followed,<br/>more a trail of thoughts from one man's mind.<br/>Although i feel this book is open to interpretation depending<br/>on what state of mind said individual is in or how they decide to approach a situation i found i could highly relate to the</span>
  <span id="freeText15668854765939446038" style="display:none">There are many things i enjoy about this book.<br />For starters, you can easily put this book down, forget<br />about it, concentrate your efforts on something else and come back to it and can start off right where you left it.<br />There isn't exactly a "plot" that needs to be followed,<br />more a trail of thoughts from one man's mind.<br />Although i feel this book is open to interpretation depending<br />on what state of mind said individual is in or how they decide to approach a situation i found i could highly relate to the emotions and thoughts of this "man", even though the setting and time is in a completely different era to mine.<br />I would recommend it to anyone who has a desire to explore the goings-on of everyday life and the passing thoughts that we delve into.<br />Certainly one you can pick up again and again and experience different thoughts and emotions to.<br />x</span>
  <a data-text-id="15668854765939446038" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_4020672" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4020672" />
    <a title="Rodney" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/218106-rodney"><img alt="Rodney" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1185330280p2/218106.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4020672?book_show_action=true">Aug 03, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rodney" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rodney" href="/user/show/218106-rodney">Rodney</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781852427580" href="/book/show/45973.The_Book_of_Disquiet">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/218106-rodney?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4020672" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10278457190685436473">For me, this translation <i>is</i> <i>The Book of Disquiet</i>, the others the shabby work of rogue heteroyms. Margaret Jull Costa renders Pessoa's langorous self-pity in a rich, clause-heavy, "European" prose that snakes through time like Scott-Moncrieff's Proust. How Zenith cornered the U.S. Pessoa market baffles me; hold out if you can for this harder-to-find edition. </span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_12935551" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781852427580" href="/book/show/45973.The_Book_of_Disquiet">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/635936-jeremy?shelf=translation">translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/635936-jeremy?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer12935551" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13718309242102655362">margaret jull costa was awarded a literary prize for translation of this book. while she continues to deftly translate some of the greatest iberian writers (jose saramago, eca de queiros, etc.), her work on this book is outdone by the richard zenith translation. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45974.The_Book_of_Disquiet' rel='nofollow noopener'> that edition</a> is far superior and more comprehensive.</span>
  <span id="freeText13718309242102655362" style="display:none">margaret jull costa was awarded a literary prize for translation of this book. while she continues to deftly translate some of the greatest iberian writers (jose saramago, eca de queiros, etc.), her work on this book is outdone by the richard zenith translation. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45974.The_Book_of_Disquiet" rel="nofollow noopener"> that edition</a> is far superior and more comprehensive.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('PSDfIy+VNQuNaCJG+MLGXtg10Xn+bJHQu8BRPD39XAMHBrbxlMv8YypDlicPN8yBDmo2sTDhH7fgvheN8L6umQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fnbgIgRPMzEJOy5BtTwWxshKnk2KH+QrDvQfh1yJTEJEUInwvxH6Wa4QmiBCyRwZHhV5hUSSakxVilk2kcq+2A==')})
  }

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_12395'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12395.Journey_to_the_End_of_the_Night?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Journey to the End of the Night<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/7869.Louis_Ferdinand_C_line?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Louis-Ferdinand Céline<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.22 avg rating &mdash; 32,139 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1932\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer12486645518359274824\">Louis-Ferdinand Celine\'s revulsion and anger at what he considered the idiocy and hypocrisy of society explodes from nearly every page of this novel. Filled with slang and obscenities and written in raw, colloquial language, Journey to the End of the<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText12486645518359274824\" style=\"display:none\">Louis-Ferdinand Celine\'s revulsion and anger at what he considered the idiocy and hypocrisy of society explodes from nearly every page of this novel. Filled with slang and obscenities and written in raw, colloquial language, Journey to the End of the Night is a literary symphony of violence, cruelty and obscene nihilism. This book shocked most critics when it was first published in France in 1932, but quickly became a success with the reading public in Europe, and later in America where it was first published by New Directions in 1952. The story of the improbable yet convincingly described travels of the petit-bourgeois (and largely autobiographical) antihero, Bardamu, from the trenches of World War I, to the African jungle, to New York and Detroit, and finally to life as a failed doctor in Paris, takes the readers by the scruff and hurtles them toward the novel\'s inevitable, sad conclusion.<\/span>\n  <a It is a long narrative prose poem which celebrates the principle of Evil in an elaborate style and with a passion akin to religious fanaticism. The French poet-critic Georges Hugnet has written of Lautréamont: &quot;He terrifies, stupefies, strikes dumb. He could look squarely at that which others had merely given a passing glance.&quot;\n\n\nLittle is known of the author of Maldoror, Isidore Ducasse, self-styled Comte de Lautréamont, except that he was born in Montevideo, Uruguay in 1846 and died in Paris at the age of twenty-four. When first published in 1868-9, Maldoror went almost unnoticed. But in the nineties the book was rediscovered and hailed as a work of genius by such eminent writers as Huysmans, Léon Bloy, Maeterlinck, and Rémy de Gourmont. Later still, Lautréamont was to be canonized as one of their principal &quot;ancestors&quot; by the Paris Surrealists.\n\n\nThis edition, translated by Guy Wernham, includes also a long introduction to a never-written, or now lost, volume of poetry.
